the day s work. 
by rudyard kipling. 
content. 
the bridge builder a walk delegate the ship that find herself the tomb of his ancestor the devil and the deep sea william the conqueror. the maltese cat bread upon the water an error in the fourth dimension my sunday at home the brushwood boy. 
the bridge builder. 
the least that findlayson of the public work department expect be a c. i. e. he dream of a c. s. i. indeed his friend tell him that he deserve more. for three year he have endure heat and cold disappointment discomfort danger and disease with responsibility almost too heavy for one pair of shoulder. and day by day through that time the great kashi bridge over the gang have grow under his charge. now in le than three month if all go well his excellency the viceroy would open the bridge in state an archbishop would bless it and the first trainload of soldier would come over it and there would be speech. 
findlayson c. e. sit in his trolley on a construction line that run along one of the main revetment the huge stone face bank that flare away north and south for three mile on either side of the river and permit himself to think of the end. with it approach his work be one mile and three quarter fin length. a lattice girder bridge truss with the findlayson truss stand on seven and twenty brick pie. each one of those pier be twenty four foot in diameter cap with red agra stone and sink eighty foot below the shift sand of the gang bed. above them be a railway line fifteen foot broad. above that again a cart road of eighteen foot flank with footpath. at either end rise tower of red brick loopholed for musketry and pierce for big gun and the ramp of the road be be push forward to their haunch. the raw earth end be crawl and alive with hundred upon hundred of tiny ass climb out of the yawn borrow pit below with sackful of stuff. and the hot afternoon air be fill with the noise of hoof the rattle of the driver stick and the swish and roll down of the dirt. the river be very low and on the dazzle white sand between the three centre pier stand squat crib of railway sleeper fill within and daub without with mud to support the last of the girder a those be rivet up. in the little deep water leave by the drought an overhead crane travel to and fro along it spile pier jerk section of iron into place snort and back and grunt a an elephant grunt in the timber yard. riveter by the hundred swarm about the lattice side work and the iron roof of the railway line hang from invisible stag under the belly of the girder cluster round the throat of the pier and ride on the overhang of the footpath stanchion. their fire pot and the spurt of flame that answer each hammer stroke show no more than pale yellow in the sun s glare. east and west and north and south the construction train rattle and shriek up and down the embankment the pile truck of brown and white stone bang behind them till the side board be unpin and with a roar and a grumble a few thousand ton more material be fling out to hold the river in place. 
findlayson c. e. turn on his trolley and look over the face of the country that he have change for seven mile around. look back on the hum village of five thousand workman. up stream and down along the vista of spur and sand. across the river to the far pier lessen in the haze. overhead to the guard tower and only he know how strong those be and with a sigh of contentment saw that his work be good. there stand his bridge before him in the sunlight lack only a few week work on the girder of the three middle pier his bridge raw and ugly a original sin but pukka permanent to endure when all memory of the builder yea even of the splendid findlayson truss have perish. practically the thing be do. 
hitchcock his assistant canter along the line on a little switch tail kabuli pony who through long practice could have trot securely over a trestle and nod to his chief. 
all but say he with a smile. 
i ve be think about it the senior answer. not half a bad job for two men be it. 
one and a half. gad what a cooper s hill cub i be when i come on the work. hitchcock felt very old in the crowd experience of the past three year that have teach him power and responsibility. 
you be rather a colt say findlayson. i wonder how you ll like go back to office work when this job s over. 
i shall hate it. say the young man and a he go on his eye follow findlayson s and he mutter isn t it damn good. 
i think we ll go up the service together findlayson say to himself. you re too good a youngster to waste on another man. cub thou wart. assistant thou art. personal assistant and at simla thou shalt be if any credit come to me out of the business. 
indeed. the burden of the work have fall altogether on findlayson and his assistant the young man whom he have choose because of his rawness to break to his own need. there be labour contractor by the half hundred fitter and riveter european borrow from the railway workshop with perhaps twenty white and half caste subordinate to direct under direction the bevy of workman but none know good than these two who trust each other how the underling be not to be trust. they have be try many time in sudden crisis by slip of boom by break of tackle failure of crane and the wrath of the river but no stress have bring to light any man among men whom findlayson and hitchcock would have honour by work a remorselessly a they work themselves. findlayson think it over from the begin. the month of office work destroy at a blow when the government of india at the last moment add two foot to the width of the bridge under the impression that bridge be cut out of paper and so bring to ruin at least half an acre of calculation and hitchcock new to disappointment bury his head in his arm and weep. the heart break delay over the fill of the contract in england. the futile correspondence hint at great wealth of commission if one only one rather doubtful consignment be pass. the war that follow the refusal. the careful polite obstruction at the other end that follow the war till young hitchcock put one month s leave to another month and borrow ten day from findlayson spend his poor little saving of a year in a wild dash to london and there a his own tongue assert and the late consignment prove put the fear of god into a man so great that he fear only parliament and say so till hitchcock work with him across his own dinner table and he fear the kashi bridge and all who speak in it name. then there be the cholera that come in the night to the village by the bridge work. and after the cholera smite the smallpox. the fever they have always with them. hitchcock have be appoint a magistrate of the third class with whip power for the good government of the community and findlayson watch him wield his power temperately learn what to overlook and what to look after. it be a long long reverie and it cover storm sudden freshet death in every manner and shape violent and awful rage against red tape half frenzying a mind that know it should be busy on other thing. drought sanitation finance. birth wed burial and riot in the village of twenty war caste. argument expostulation persuasion and the blank despair that a man go to bed upon thankful that his rifle be all in piece in the gun case. behind everything rise the black frame of the kashi bridge plate by plate girder by girder span by span and each pier of it recall hitchcock the all round man who have stand by his chief without fail from the very first to this last. 
so the bridge be two men s work unless one count peroo a peroo certainly count himself. he be a lascar a kharva from bulsar familiar with every port between rockhampton and london who have rise to the rank of sarang on the british india boat but weary of routine muster and clean clothe have throw up the service and go inland where men of his calibre be sure of employment. for his knowledge of tackle and the handle of heavy weight peroo be worth almost any price he might have choose to put upon his service. but custom decree the wage of the overhead men and peroo be not within many silver piece of his proper value. neither run water nor extreme height make him afraid. and a an ex serang he know how to hold authority. no piece of iron be so big or so badly place that peroo could not devise a tackle to lift it a loose end sag arrangement rig with a scandalous amount of talk but perfectly equal to the work in hand. it be peroo who have save the girder of number seven pier from destruction when the new wire rope jam in the eye of the crane and the huge plate tilt in it sling threaten to slide out sideways. then the native workman lose their head with great shouting and hitchcock s right arm be break by a fall t plate and he button it up in his coat and swoon and come to and direct for four hour till peroo from the top of the crane report all s well and the plate swing home. there be no one like peroo serang to lash and guy and hold to control the donkey engine to hoist a fall locomotive craftily out of the borrow pit into which it have tumble. to strip and dive if need be to see how the concrete block round the pier stand the scour of mother gunga or to adventure up stream on a monsoon night and report on the state of the embankment facing. he would interrupt the field council of findlayson and hitchcock without fear till his wonderful english or his still more wonderful lingua franca half portuguese and half malay run out and he be force to take string and show the knot that he would recommend. he control his own gang of tacklemen mysterious relative from kutch mandvi gather month by month and try to the uttermost. no consideration of family or kin allow peroo to keep weak hand or a giddy head on the pay roll. my honour be the honour of this bridge he would say to the about to be dismiss. what do i care for your honour. go and work on a steamer. that be all you be fit for. 
the little cluster of hut where he and his gang live centre round the tattered dwell of a sea priest one who have never set foot on black water but have be choose a ghostly counsellor by two generation of sea rover all unaffected by port mission or those creed which be thrust upon sailor by agency along thames bank. the priest of the lascara have nothing to do with their caste or indeed with anything at all. he eat the offering of his church and sleep and smoke and sleep again for say peroo who have hale him a thousand mile inland he be a very holy man. he never care what you eat so long a you do not eat beef and that be good because on land we worship shiva we kharvas. but at sea on the kumpani s boat we attend strictly to the order of the burra malum [the first mate] and on this bridge we observe what finlinson sahib say. 
finlinson sahib have that day give order to clear the scaffold from the guard tower on the right bank and peroo with his mate be cast loose and lower down the bamboo pole and plank a swiftly a ever they have whip the cargo out of a coaster. 
from his trolley he could hear the whistle of the serang s silver pipe and the creak and clatter of the pulley. peroo be stand on the topmost cop of the tower clothe in the blue dungaree of his abandon service and a findlayson motion to him to be careful for his be no life to throw away he grip the last pole and shade his eye ship fashion answer with the long draw wail of the fo c sle lookout. ham dekhta hai. i be look out. findlayson laugh and then sigh. it be year since he have see a steamer and he be sick for home. a his trolley pass under the tower peroo descend by a rope ape fashion and cry. it look well now sahib. our bridge be all but do. what think you mother gunga will say when the rail run over. 
she ha say little so far. it be never mother gunga that delay u. 
there be always time for her. and none the le there ha be delay. ha the sahib forget last autumn s flood when the stoneboats be sink without warn or only a half day s warn. 
yes but nothing save a big flood could hurt u now. the spur be hold well on the west bank. 
mother gunga eat great allowance. there be always room for more stone on the revetment. i tell this to the chota sahib he mean hitchcock and he laugh. 
no matter peroo. another year thou wilt be able to build a bridge in thine own fashion. 
the lascar grin. then it will not be in this way with stonework sink under water a the quetta be sink. i like sus suspen sheen bridge that fly from bank to bank with one big step like a gang plank. then no water can hurt. when doe the lord sahib come to open the bridge. 
in three month when the weather be cool. 
ho. ho. he be like the burra malum. he sleep below while the work be be do. then he come upon the quarter deck and touch with his finger and say. this be not clean. dam jibboonwallah. 
but the lord sahib doe not call me a dam jibboonwallah peroo. 
no sahib. but he doe not come on deck till the work be all finish. even the burra malum of the nerbudda say once at tuticorin. 
bah. go. i be busy. 
i also. say peroo with an unshaken countenance. may i take the light dinghy now and row along the spur. 
to hold them with thy hand. they be i think sufficiently heavy. 
nay sahib. it be thus. at sea on the black water we have room to be blow up and down without care. here we have no room at all. look you we have put the river into a dock and run her between stone sill. 
findlayson smile at the we. 
we have bitted and bridle her. she be not like the sea that can beat against a soft beach. she be mother gunga in iron. his voice fell a little. 
peroo thou hast be up and down the world more even than i. speak true talk now. how much dolt thou in thy heart believe of mother gunga. 
all that our priest say. london be london sahib. sydney be sydney and port darwin be port darwin. also mother gunga be mother gunga and when i come back to her bank i know this and worship. in london i do poojah to the big temple by the river for the sake of the god within. yes i will not take the cushion in the dinghy. 
findlayson mount his horse and trot to the shed of a bungalow that he share with his assistant. the place have become home to him in the last three year. he have grill in the heat sweat in the rain and shiver with fever under the rude thatch roof. the lime wash beside the door be cover with rough drawing and formula and the sentry path tread in the mat of the verandah show where he have walk alone. there be no eight hour limit to an engineer s work and the even meal with hitchcock be eat boot and spur. over their cigar they listen to the hum of the village a the gang come up from the river bed and the light begin to twinkle. 
peroo ha go up the spur in your dinghy. he s take a couple of nephew with him and he s loll in the stern like a commodore say hitchcock. 
that s all right. he s get something on his mind. you d think that ten year in the british india boat would have knock most of his religion out of him. 
so it ha say hitchcock chuckle. i overhear him the other day in the middle of a most atheistical talk with that fat old guru of theirs. peroo deny the efficacy of prayer. and want the guru to go to sea and watch a gale out with him and see if he could stop a monsoon. 
all the same if you carry off his guru he d leave u like a shoot. he be yarn away to me about pray to the dome of st. paul s when he be in london. 
he tell me that the first time he go into the engine room of a steamer when he be a boy he pray to the low pressure cylinder. 
not half a bad thing to pray to either. he s propitiate his own god now and he want to know what mother gunga will think of a bridge be run across her. who s there. a shadow darken the doorway and a telegram be put into hitchcock s hand. 
she ought to be pretty well use to it by this time. only a tar. it ought to be ralli s answer about the new rivet. great heaven. hitchcock jump to his foot. 
what be it. say the senior and take the form. that s what mother gunga think be it he say read. keep cool young un. we ve get all our work cut out for u. let s see. muir wire half an hour ago. flood on the ramgunga. look out. well that give u one two nine and a half for the flood to reach melipur ghaut and seven s sixteen and a half to lataoli say fifteen hour before it come down to u. 
curse that hill feed sewer of a ramgunga. findlayson this be two month before anything could have be expect and the leave bank be litter up with stuff still. two full month before the time. 
that s why it come. i ve only know indian river for five and twenty year and i don t pretend to understand. here come another tar. findlayson open the telegram. cockran this time from the gang canal. heavy rain here. bad. he might have save the last word. well we don t want to know any more. we ve get to work the gang all night and clean up the river bed. you ll take the east bank and work out to meet me in the middle. get every thing that float below the bridge. we shall have quite enough rivercraft come down adrift anyhow without let the stone boat ram the pier. what have you get on the east bank that need look after. 
pontoon one big pontoon with the overhead crane on it. t other overhead crane on the mend pontoon with the cart road rivet from twenty to twenty three pier two construction line and a turn spur. the pilework must take it chance say hitchcock. 
all right. roll up everything you can lay hand on. we ll give the gang fifteen minute more to eat their grub. 
close to the verandah stand a big night gong never use except for flood or fire in the village. hitchcock have call for a fresh horse and be off to his side of the bridge when findlayson take the cloth bind stick and smite with the rub stroke that bring out the full thunder of the metal. 
long before the last rumble cease every night gong in the village have take up the warn. to these be add the hoarse scream of conch in the little temple. the throb of drum and tom tom. and from the european quarter where the riveter live mccartney s bugle a weapon of offence on sunday and festival bray desperately call to stable. engine after engine toil home along the spur at the end of her day s work whistle in answer till the whistle be answer from the far bank. then the big gong thunder thrice for a sign that it be flood and not fire. conch drum and whistle echo the call and the village quiver to the sound of bare foot run upon soft earth. the order in all case be to stand by the day s work and wait instruction. the gang pour by in the dusk. men stop to knot a loin cloth or fasten a sandal. gang foreman shout to their subordinate a they run or pause by the tool issue shed for bar and mattock. locomotive creep down their track wheel deep in the crowd. till the brown torrent disappear into the dusk of the river bed race over the pilework swarm along the lattice cluster by the crane and stand still each man in his place. 
then the trouble beat of the gong carry the order to take up everything and bear it beyond highwater mark and the flare lamp break out by the hundred between the web of dull iron a the riveter begin a night s work race against the flood that be to come. the girder of the three centre pier those that stand on the crib be all but in position. they need just a many rivet a could be drive into them for the flood would assuredly wash out their support and the ironwork would settle down on the cap of stone if they be not block at the end. a hundred crowbar strain at the sleeper of the temporary line that feed the unfinished pier. it be heave up in length load into truck and back up the bank beyond flood level by the groan locomotive. the tool shed on the sand melt away before the attack of shout army and with them go the stack rank of government store iron bind box of rivet plier cutter duplicate part of the rivet machine spare pump and chain. the big crane would be the last to be shift for she be hoist all the heavy stuff up to the main structure of the bridge. the concrete block on the fleet of stone boat be drop overside where there be any depth of water to guard the pier and the empty boat themselves be pole under the bridge down stream. it be here that peroo s pipe shrill loud for the first stroke of the big gong have bring the dinghy back at race speed and peroo and his people be strip to the waist work for the honour and credit which be good than life. 
i know she would speak he cry. i know but the telegraph give u good warn. o son of unthinkable beget child of unspeakable shame be we here for the look of the thing. it be two foot of wire rope fray at the end and it do wonder a peroo leap from gunnel to gunnel shout the language of the sea. 
findlayson be more trouble for the stone boat than anything else. mccartney with his gang be block up the end of the three doubtful span but boat adrift if the flood chance to be a high one might endanger the girder. and there be a very fleet in the shrink channel. 
get them behind the swell of the guard tower he shout down to peroo. it will be dead water there. get them below the bridge. 
accha. [very good. ] i know. we be moor them with wire rope be the answer. heh. i listen to the chota sahib. he be work hard. 
from across the river come an almost continuous whistle of locomotive back by the rumble of stone. hitchcock at the last minute be spend a few hundred more truck of tarakee stone in reinforce his spur and embankment. 
the bridge challenge mother gunga say peroo with a laugh. but when she talk i know whose voice will be the loud. 
for hour the naked men work scream and shout under the light. it be a hot moonless night. the end of it be darken by cloud and a sudden squall that make findlayson very grave. 
she move. say peroo just before the dawn. mother gunga be awake. hear. he dip his hand over the side of a boat and the current mumble on it. a little wave hit the side of a pier with a crisp slap. 
six hour before her time say findlayson mop his forehead savagely. now we can t depend on anything. we d good clear all hand out of the river bed. 
again the big gong beat and a second time there be the rush of naked foot on earth and ring iron. the clatter of tool cease. in the silence men hear the dry yawn of water crawl over thirsty sand. 
foreman after foreman shout to findlayson who have post himself by the guard tower that his section of the river bed have be clean out and when the last voice drop findlayson hurry over the bridge till the iron plat of the permanent way give place to the temporary plank walk over the three centre pier and there he meet hitchcock. 
all clear your side. say findlayson. the whisper ring in the box of latticework. 
yes and the east channel s fill now. we re utterly out of our reckon. when be this thing down on u. 
there s no say. she s fill a fast a she can. look. findlayson point to the plank below his foot where the sand burn and defile by month of work be begin to whisper and fizz. 
what order. say hitchcock. 
call the roll count store sit on your hunker and pray for the bridge. that s all i can think of. good night. don t risk your life try to fish out anything that may go down stream. 
oh i ll be a prudent a you be. night. heaven how she s fill. here s the rain in earnest. findlayson pick his way back to his bank sweep the last of mccartney s riveter before him. the gang have spread themselves along the embankment regardless of the cold rain of the dawn and there they wait for the flood. only peroo keep his men together behind the swell of the guard tower where the stone boat lay tie fore and aft with hawser wire rope and chain. 
a shrill wail run along the line grow to a yell half fear and half wonder. the face of the river whiten from bank to bank between the stone facing and the faraway spur go out in spout of foam. mother gunga have come bank high in haste and a wall of chocolate colour water be her messenger. there be a shriek above the roar of the water the complaint of the span come down on their block a the crib be whirl out from under their belly. the stone boat groan and grind each other in the eddy that swing round the abutment and their clumsy mast rise high and high against the dim sky line. 
before she be shut between these wall we know what she would do. now she be thus cramp god only know what she will do. say peroo watch the furious turmoil round the guard tower. ohe. fight then. fight hard for it be thus that a woman wear herself out. 
but mother gunga would not fight a peroo desire. after the first down stream plunge there come no more wall of water but the river lift herself bodily a a snake when she drink in midsummer pluck and finger along the revetment and bank up behind the pier till even findlayson begin to recalculate the strength of his work. 
when day come the village gasp. only last night men say turn to each other it be a a town in the river bed. look now. 
and they look and wonder afresh at the deep water the race water that lick the throat of the pier. the farther bank be veil by rain into which the bridge run out and vanish. the spur up stream be mark by no more than eddy and spout and down stream the pen river once free of her guide line have spread like a sea to the horizon. then hurry by roll in the water dead men and ox together with here and there a patch of thatch roof that melt when it touch a pier. 
big flood say peroo and findlayson nod. it be a big a flood a he have any wish to watch. his bridge would stand what be upon her now but not very much more and if by any of a thousand chance there happen to be a weakness in the embankment mother gunga would carry his honour to the sea with the other raffle. bad of all there be nothing to do except to sit still. and findlayson sit still under his macintosh till his helmet become pulp on his head and his boot be over ankle in mire. he take no count of time for the river be mark the hour inch by inch and foot by foot along the embankment and he listen numb and hungry to the strain of the stone boat the hollow thunder under the pier and the hundred noise that make the full note of a flood. once a drip servant bring him food but he could not eat. and once he think that he hear a faint toot from a locomotive across the river and then he smile. the bridge s failure would hurt his assistant not a little but hitchcock be a young man with his big work yet to do. for himself the crash mean everything everything that make a hard life worth the live. they would say the men of his own profession. he remember the half pity thing that he himself have say when lockhart s new waterworks burst and break down in brickheaps and sludge and lockhart s spirit break in him and he die. he remember what he himself have say when the sumao bridge go out in the big cyclone by the sea. and most he remember poor hartopp s face three week late when the shame have mark it. his bridge be twice the size of hartopp s and it carry the findlayson truss a well a the new pier shoe the findlayson bolt shoe. there be no excuse in his service. government might listen perhaps but his own kind would judge him by his bridge a that stand or fell. he go over it in his head plate by plate span by span brick by brick pier by pier remember compare estimate and recalculate l there should be any mistake. and through the long hour and through the flight of formula that dance and wheel before him a cold fear would come to pinch his heart. his side of the sum be beyond question. but what man know mother gunga s arithmetic. even a he be make all sure by the multiplication table the river might be scoop a pot hole to the very bottom of any one of those eighty foot pier that carry his reputation. again a servant come to him with food but his mouth be dry and he could only drink and return to the decimal in his brain. and the river be still rise. peroo in a mat shelter coat crouch at his foot watch now his face and now the face of the river but say nothing. 
at last the lascar rise and flounder through the mud towards the village but he be careful to leave an ally to watch the boat. 
presently he return most irreverently drive before him the priest of his creed a fat old man with a grey beard that whip the wind with the wet cloth that blow over his shoulder. never be see so lamentable a guru. 
what good be offering and little kerosene lamp and dry grain shout peroo if squat in the mud be all that thou canst do. thou hast deal long with the god when they be content and well wish. now they be angry. speak to them. 
what be a man against the wrath of god. whine the priest cower a the wind take him. let me go to the temple and i will pray there. 
son of a pig pray here. be there no return for salt fish and curry powder and dry onion. call aloud. tell mother gunga we have have enough. bid her be still for the night. i cannot pray but i have be serve in the kumpani s boat and when men do not obey my order i a flourish of the wire rope colt round the sentence and the priest break free from his disciple flee to the village. 
fat pig. say peroo. after all that we have do for him. when the flood be down i will see to it that we get a new guru. finlinson sahib it darken for night now and since yesterday nothing ha be eat. be wise sahib. no man can endure watch and great think on an empty belly. lie down sahib. the river will do what the river will do. 
the bridge be mine. i cannot leave it. 
wilt thou hold it up with thy hand then. say peroo laugh. i be trouble for my boat and sheer before the flood come. now we be in the hand of the god. the sahib will not eat and lie down. take these then. they be meat and good toddy together and they kill all weariness besides the fever that follow the rain. i have eat nothing else to day at all. 
he take a small tin tobacco box from his sodden waistbelt and thrust it into findlayson s hand say nay do not be afraid. it be no more than opium clean malwa opium. 
findlayson shake two or three of the dark brown pellet into his hand and hardly know what he do swallow them. the stuff be at least a good guard against fever the fever that be creep upon him out of the wet mud and he have see what peroo could do in the stew mist of autumn on the strength of a dose from the tin box. 
peroo nod with bright eye. in a little in a little the sahib will find that he think well again. i too will he dive into his treasure box resettle the rain coat over his head and squat down to watch the boat. it be too dark now to see beyond the first pier and the night seem to have give the river new strength. findlayson stand with his chin on his chest think. there be one point about one of the pier the seventh that he have not fully settle in his mind. the figure would not shape themselves to the eye except one by one and at enormous interval of time. there be a sound rich and mellow in his ear like the deep note of a double bass an entrance sound upon which he ponder for several hour a it seem. then peroo be at his elbow shout that a wire hawser have snap and the stone boat be loose. findlayson saw the fleet open and swing out fanwise to a long draw shriek of wire strain across gunnel. 
a tree hit them. they will all go cry peroo. the main hawser ha part. what doe the sahib do. 
an immensely complex plan have suddenly flash into findlayson s mind. he saw the rope run from boat to boat in straight line and angle each rope a line of white fire. but there be one rope which be the master rope. he could see that rope. if he could pull it once it be absolutely and mathematically certain that the disorder fleet would reassemble itself in the backwater behind the guard tower. but why he wonder be peroo cling so desperately to his waist a he hasten down the bank. it be necessary to put the lascar aside gently and slowly because it be necessary to save the boat and further to demonstrate the extreme ease of the problem that look so difficult. and then but it be of no conceivable importance a wirerope race through his hand burn it the high bank disappear and with it all the slowly disperse factor of the problem. he be sit in the rainy darkness sit in a boat that spin like a top and peroo be stand over him. 
i have forget say the lascar slowly that to those fast and unused the opium be bad than any wine. those who die in gunga go to the god. still i have no desire to present myself before such great one. can the sahib swim. 
what need. he can fly fly a swiftly a the wind be the thick answer. 
he be mad. mutter peroo under his breath. and he throw me aside like a bundle of dung cake. well he will not know his death. the boat cannot live an hour here even if she strike nothing. it be not good to look at death with a clear eye. 
he refresh himself again from the tin box squat down in the bow of the reel peg and stitch craft star through the mist at the nothing that be there. a warm drowsiness creep over findlayson the chief engineer whose duty be with his bridge. the heavy raindrop strike him with a thousand tingle little thrill and the weight of all time since time be make hang heavy on his eyelid. he think and perceive that he be perfectly secure for the water be so solid that a man could surely step out upon it and stand still with his leg apart to keep his balance this be the most important point would be bear with great and easy speed to the shore. but yet a good plan come to him. it need only an exertion of will for the soul to hurl the body ashore a wind drive paper to waft it kite fashion to the bank. thereafter the boat spin dizzily suppose the high wind get under the free body. would it tower up like a kite and pitch headlong on the far away sand or would it duck about beyond control through all eternity. findlayson grip the gunnel to anchor himself for it seem that he be on the edge of take the flight before he have settle all his plan. opium ha more effect on the white man than the black. peroo be only comfortably indifferent to accident. she cannot live he grunt. her seam open already. if she be even a dinghy with oar we could have ride it out. but a box with hole be no good. finlinson sahib she fill.

a walk delegate. 
accord to the custom of vermont sunday afternoon be salt time on the farm and unless something very important happen we attend to the salt ourselves. dave and pete the red ox be treat first. they stay in the home meadow ready for work on monday. then come the cow with pan the calf who should have be turn into veal long ago but survive on account of his manner. and lastly the horse scatter through the seventy acre of the back pasture. 
you must go down by the brook that feed the click bubble water ram. up through the sugar bush where the young maple undergrowth close round you like a shallow sea. next follow the faint line of an old county road run past two green hollow fringe with wild rise that mark the cellar of two ruin house. then by lose orchard where nobody ever come except in cider time. then across another brook and so into the back pasture. half of it be pine and hemlock and spruce with sumach and little juniper bush and the other half be grey rock and boulder and moss with green streak of brake and swamp. but the horse like it well enough our own and the others that be turn down there to fee at fifty cent a week. most people walk to the back pasture and find it very rough work. but one can get there in a buggy if the horse know what be expect of him. the safe conveyance be our coupe. this begin life a a buckboard and we buy it for five dollar from a sorrowful man who have no other sort of possession. and the seat come off one night when we be turn a corner in a hurry. after that alteration it make a beautiful salt machine if you hold tight because there be nothing to catch your foot when you fell out and the slat rattle tune. 
one sunday afternoon we go out with the salt a usual. it be a broil hot day and we could not find the horse anywhere till we let tedda gabler the bobtailed mare who throw up the dirt with her big hoof exactly a a tedder throw hay have her head. clever a she be she tip the coupe over in a hide brook before she come out on a ledge of rock where all the horse have gather and be switch fly. the deacon be the first to call to her. he be a very dark iron grey four year old son of grandee. he ha be handle since he be two be drive in a light cart before he be three and now rank a an absolutely steady lady s horse proof against steam roller grade crossing and street procession. 
salt. say the deacon joyfully. you re dreffle late tedda. 
any any place to cramp the coupe. tedda pant. it weigh turr ble this weather. i d a come sooner but they didn t know what they want ner haow. fell out twice both of em. i don t understand sech foolishness. 
you look consider ble het up. guess you d good cramp her under them pine an cool off a piece. 
tedda scramble on the ledge and cramp the coupe in the shade of a tiny little wood of pine while my companion and i lay down among the brown silky needle and gasp. all the home horse be gather round u enjoy their sunday leisure. 
there be rod and rick the senior on the farm. they be the regular road pair bay with black point full brother age son of a hambletonian sire and a morgan dam. there be nip and tuck seal brown rise six brother and sister black hawk by birth perfectly match just finish their education and a handsome a pair a man could wish to find in a forty mile drive. there be muldoon our ex car horse buy at a venture and any colour you choose that be not white. and tweezy who come from kentucky with an affliction of his leave hip which make him a little uncertain how his hind leg be move. he and muldoon have be haul gravel all the week for our new road. the deacon you know already. last of all and eat something be our faithful marcus aurelius antoninus the black buggy horse who have see u through every state of weather and road the horse who be always stand in harness before some door or other a philosopher with the appetite of a shark and the manner of an archbishop. tedda gabler be a new trade with a reputation for vice which be really the result of bad drive. she have one work gait which she could hold till further notice. a roman nose. a large prominent eye. a shave brush of a tail. and an irritable temper. she take her salt through her bridle. but the others trot up nuzzle and wickering for theirs till we empty it on the clean rock. they be all stand at ease on three leg for the most part talk the ordinary gossip of the back pasture about the scarcity of water and gap in the fence and how the early windfall taste that season when little rick blow the last few grain of his allowance into a crevice and say. 
hurry boy. might ha know that livery plug would be around. 
we hear a clatter of hoof and there climb up from the ravine below a fifty center transient a wall eye yellow frame house of a horse send up to board from a livery stable in town where they call him the lamb and never let him out except at night and to stranger. my companion who know and have break most of the horse look at the rag hammer head a it rise and say quietly. 
ni ice beast. man eater if he get the chance see his eye. kicker too see his hock. western horse. 
the animal lumber up snuffle and grunt. his foot show that he have not work for week and week and our creature draw together significantly. 
a usual he say with an underhung sneer bowin your head before the oppressor that come to spend his leisure gloatin over you. 
mine s do say the deacon. he lick up the remnant of his salt drop his nose in his master s hand and sing a little grace all to himself. the deacon ha the most enchant manner of any one i know. 
an fawnin on them for what be your inalienable right. it s humiliatin say the yellow horse sniff to see if he could find a few spare grain. 
go daown hill then boney the deacon reply. guess you ll find somethin to eat still if yer hain t hog it all. you ve ett more n any three of u to day an day fore that an the last two month sence you ve be here. 
i be not addressin myself to the young an immature. i be speakin to those whose opinion an experience command respect. 
i saw rod raise his head a though he be about to make a remark. then he drop it again and stand three corner like a plough horse. rod can cover his mile in a shade under three minute on an ordinary road to an ordinary buggy. he be tremendously powerful behind but like most hambletonians he grow a trifle sullen a he get old. no one can love rod very much. but no one can help respect him. 
i wish to wake those the yellow horse go on to an abidin sense o their wrong an their injury an their outrage. 
haow s that. say marcus aurelius antoninus dreamily. he think boney be talk of some kind of fee. 
an when i say outrage and injury boney wave his tail furiously i mean em too. great oat. that s just what i do mean plain an straight. 
the gentleman talk quite earnest say tuck the mare to nip her brother. there s no doubt thinkin broaden the horizon o the mind. his language be quite lofty. 
hesh si nip answer. 
he hain t widen nothin cep the circle he s ett in pasture. they fee word fer beddin where he come from. 
it s elegant talkin though tuck return with an unconvinced toss of her pretty lean little head. 
the yellow horse hear her and strike an attitude which he mean to be extremely impressive. it make him look a though he have be badly stuff. 
now i ask you i ask you without prejudice an without favour what ha man the oppressor ever do for you. be you not inalienably entitle to the free air o heaven blowin acrost this boundless prairie. 
hev ye ever winter here. say the deacon merrily while the others snicker. it s kind cool. 
not yet say boney. i come from the boundless confine o kansa where the noble of our kind have their abidin place among the sunflower on the threshold o the settin sun in his glory. 
an they send you ahead a a sample. say rick with an amuse quiver of his long beautifully groom tail a thick and a fine and a wavy a a quadroon s back hair. 
kansa sir need no advertisement. her native son rely on themselves an their native sire. yes sir. 
then tweezy lift up his wise and polite old head. his affliction make him bashful a a rule but he be ever the most courteous of horse. 
excuse me suh he say slowly but unless i have be misinfohmed most of your prominent siahs suh be impo ted from kentucky. an i m from paduky. 
there be the least little touch of pride in the last word. 
any horse dat know bean say muldoon suddenly. he have be stand with his hairy chin on tweezy s broad quarter. git out kansa fore dey crip his shoe. i blow in dere from ioway in de day o me youth an innocence an i wuz grateful when dey box me fer n york. you can t tell me anything about kansa i don t wanter fergit. de belt line stable ain t no hoffman house but dey re vanderbilt longside o kansa. 
what the horse o kansa think to day the horse of america will think to morrow. an i tell you that when the horse of america rise in their might the day o the oppressor be end. 
there be a pause till rick say with a little grunt. 
ef you put it that way every one of u ha riz in his might cep marcus mebbe. marky j ever rise in yer might. 
nope say marcus aurelius antoninus thoughtfully quidding over a mouthful of grass. i see a heap o fool try though. 
you admit that you riz. say the kansa horse excitedly. then why why in kansa do you ever go under again. 
horse can t walk on his hind leg all the time say the deacon. 
not when he s jerk over on his back fore he know what fetch him. we ve all do it boney say rick. nip an tuck they try it spite o what the deacon tell em. an the deacon he try it spite o what me an rod tell him. an me an rod try it spite o what grandee tell u. an i guess grandee he try it spite o what his dam tell him. it s the same old circus from generation to generation. colt can t see why he s call on to back. same old rearm on end straight up. same old feelin that you ve best em this time. same old little yank at your mouth when you re up good an tall. same old pegasus act wonderin where you ll light. same old wop when you hit the dirt with your head where your tail should be and your in ards shake up like a bran mash. same old voice in your ear. waal ye little fool an what do you reckon to make by that. we re through with risin in our might on this farm. we go to pole er single accordin ez we re hitch. 
an man the oppressor set an gloat over you same a he s settin now. hain t that be your experience madam. 
this last remark be address to tedda. and any one could see with half an eye that poor old anxious fidgety tedda stamp at the fly must have leave a wild and tumultuous youth behind her. 
pends on the man she answer shift from one foot to the other and address herself to the home horse. they abuse me dreffle when i be young. i guess i be sperrity an nervous some but they didn t allow for that. twas in monroe county noo york an sence then till i come here i ve run away with more men than u d fill a boardin house. why the man that sell me here he say to the bos s he. mind now i ve warn you. twon t be none of my fault if she shed you daown the road. don t you drive her in a top buggy ner thout winker s he ner think this bite ef you look to come home behind her. n the fust thing the bos do be to git the top buggy. 
can t say a i like top buggy say rick. they don t balance good. 
suit me to a ha ar say marcus aurelius antoninus. top buggy mean the baby s in behind an i kin stop while she gather the pretty flower yes an pick a maouthful too. the woman folk all say i hev to be humour an i don t kerry thing to the sweatin point. 
course i ve no prejudice against a top buggy s long s i can see it tedda go on quickly. it s ha f seein the pesky thing bobbin an balancn behind the winker git on my nerve. then the bos look at the bite they d sell with me an s he. jiminy christmas. this u d make a clothe horse stan n end. then he give me a plain bar bite an fit it s if there be some feelin to my maouth. 
hain t ye get any miss tedda. say tuck who ha a mouth like velvet and know it. 
might a have miss tuck but i ve forget. then he give me an open bridle my style s an open bridle an i dunno a i ought to tell this by right he give me a kiss. 
my. say tuck i can t tell fer the shoe o me what make some men so fresh. 
pshaw si say nip what s the sense in actin so. you git a kiss reg lar s hitchin up time. 
well you needn t tell smarty say tuck with a squeal and a kick. 
i d hear o kiss o course tedda go on but they hadn t come my way specially. i don t mind tellin i be that take aback at that man s doin s he might ha light fire cracker on my saddle. then we go out jest s if a kiss be nothin an i wasn t three stride into my gait fore i felt the bos knoo his business an be trustin me. so i study to please him an he never take the whip from the dash a whip drive me plumb distract an the upshot be that waal i ve come up the back pasture to day an the coupe s tip clear over twice an i ve wait till twuz fix each time. you kin judge for yourselves. i don t set up to be no good than my neighbour specially with my tail snip off the way ti but i want you all to know tedda s quit fightin in harness or out of it cep when there s a bear fool in the pasture stuffin his stummick with board that ain t rightly hisn cause he hain t earn it. 
meanin me madam. say the yellow horse. 
ef the shoe fit clinch it say tedda snort. i name no name though to be sure some folk be mean enough an greedy enough to do thout em. 
there s a deal to be forgive to ignorance say the yellow horse with an ugly look in his blue eye. 
seemin ly yes. or some folk u d ha be kick raound the pasture bout onct a minute sence they come board er no board. 
but what you do not understand if you will excuse me madam be that the whole principle o servitood which include keep an fee start from a radically false basis. an i be proud to say that me an the majority o the horse o kansa think the entire concern should be relegate to the limbo of explode superstition. i say we re too progressive for that. i say we re too enlighten for that. twas good enough s long s we didn t think but naow but naow a new loominary ha arise on the horizon. 
meanin you. say the deacon. 
the horse o kansa be behind me with their multitoodinous thunderin hoof an we say simply but grandly that we take our stand with all four foot on the inalienable right of the horse pure and simple the high tone child o nature feed by the same wavin grass cool by the same ripplin brook yes an warm by the same gen rous sun a fall impartially on the outside an the inside of the pamper machine o the trottin track or the bloat coupe horse o these yere eastern city. be we not the same flesh an blood. 
not by a bushel an a half say the deacon under his breath. grandee never be in kansa. 
my. ain t that elegant though abaout the wavin grass an the ripplin brook. tuck whisper in nip s ear. the gentleman s real convincin i think. 
i say we be the same flesh an blood. be we to be separate horse from horse by the artificial barrier of a trottin record or be we to look down upon each other on the strength o the gift o nature an extry inch below the knee or slightly more powerful quarter. what s the use o them advantage to you. man the oppressor come along an see you re likely an good lookin an grind you to the face o the earth. what for. for his own pleasure. for his own convenience. young an old black an bay white an grey there s no distinction make between u. we re grind up together under the remorseless teeth o the engine of oppression. 
guess his breechin must ha break goin daown hill say the deacon. slippery road maybe an the buggy come onter him an he didn t know nough to hold back. that don t feel like teeth though. maybe he bust a shaft an it prick him. 
an i come to you from kansa wavin the tail o friendship to all an sundry an in the name of the uncounted million o pure mind high tone horse now strugglin towards the light o freedom i say to you rub nose with u in our sacred an holy cause. the power be yourn. without you i say man the oppressor cannot move himself from place to place. without you he cannot reap he cannot sow he cannot plough. 
mighty odd place kansa. say marcus aurelius antoninus. seemin ly they reap in the spring an plough in the fall. guess it s right fer them but twould make me kind giddy. 
the produc s of your untirin industry would rot on the grind if you do not weakly consent to help him. let em rot i say. let him call you to the stable in vain an nevermore. let him shake his ensnarin oat under your nose in vain. let the brahma roost in the buggy an the rat run riot round the reaper. let him walk on his two hind foot till they blame well drop off. win no more soul destroyn race for his pleasure. then an not till then will man the oppressor know where he s at. quit workin fellow sufferer an slave. kick. rear. plunge. lie down on the shaft an woller. smash an destroy. the conflict will be but short an the victory be certain. after that we can press our inalienable right to eight quart o oat a day two good blanket an a fly net an the best o stablin. 
the yellow horse shut his yellow teeth with a triumphant snap. and tuck say with a sigh. seem s if somethin ought to be do. don t seem right somehow oppressin u an all to my way o thinkin. 
say muldoon in a far away and sleepy voice. 
who in vermont s goin to haul de inalienable oat. dey weigh like sam hill an sixty bushel at dat allowance ain t goin to last t ree week here. an dere s de winter hay for five mont s. 
we can settle those minor detail when the great cause be win say the yellow horse. let u return simply but grandly to our inalienable right the right o freedom on these yere verdant hill an no invijjus distinction o track an pedigree. 
what in stable jer call an invijjus distinction. say the deacon stiffly. 
fer one thing bein a bloat pamper trotter jest because you happen to be raise that way an couldn t no more help trottin than eatin. 
do ye know anythin about trotter. say the deacon. 
i ve see em trot. that be enough for me. i don t want to know any more. trottin s immoral. 
waal i ll tell you this much. they don t bloat an they don t pamp much. i don t hold out to be no trotter myself though i be free to say i have hope that way onct. but i do say fer i ve see em train that a trotter don t trot with his foot. he trot with his head. an he doe more work ef you know what that be in a week than you er your sire ever do in all your life. he s everlastingly at it a trotter be. an when he isn t he s studyin haow. you see em trot. much you hev. you be hitch to a rail back o the stand in a buckboard with a soap box nail on the slat an a frowzy buff lo atop while your man peddle rum fer lemonade to little boy a think they be actin manly till you be both run off the track an jail you intoed shufflin sway back wind suckin skate you. 
don t get het up deacon say tweezy quietly. now suh would you consider a fox trot an single foot an rack an pace an amble distinction not worth distinguishin. i assuah you gentleman there be a time befo i be afflict in my hip if you ll pardon me miss tuck when i be quite celebrate in paduky for all those gait. an in my opinion the deacon s co rect when he say that a ho se of any position in society get his gait by his haid an not by his ah limb miss tuck. i reckon i m very little good now but i m rememberin the thing i use to do befo i take to transpo tin real estate with the help an assistance of this gentleman here. he look at muldoon. 
invijjus arterficial hind leg. say the ex carhorse with a grunt of contempt. on de belt line we don t reckon no horse wuth his keep le he kin switch de car off de track run her round on de cobble an dump her in ag in ahead o de truck what s blockin him. dere be a way o swingin yer quarter when de driver say yank her out boy. dat take a year to learn. onct yer git onter it youse kin yank a cable car out a manhole. i don t advertise myself for no circus horse but i know dat trick good than most an dey be good to me in de stable fer i save time on de belt an time s what dey hunt in n york. 
but the simple child o nature the yellow horse begin. 
oh go an unscrew yer splint. you re talkin through yer bandage say muldoon with a horse laugh. dere ain t no loose box for de simple child o nature on de belt line wid de paris comin in an de teutonic goin out an de truck an de coupe s sayin thing an de heavy freight movin down fer de boston boat bout t ree o clock of an august afternoon in de middle of a hot wave when de fat kanucks an western horse drop dead on de block. de simple child o nature have good chase himself inter de water. every man at de end of his line be mad or load or silly an de cop s mad an loaded an silly than de rest. dey all take it out de horse. dere s no wavin brook ner ripplin grass on de belt line. run her out on de cobble wid de spark flyin an stop when de cop slug you on de bone o yer nose. dat s n york. see. 
i be always tell s ciety in noo york be dreffle refine an high tone say tuck. we re lookin to go there one o these day nip an me. 
oh you win t see no belt business where you ll go miss. de man dat want you ll want bad an he ll summer you on long island er at newport wid a winky pinky silver harness an an english coachman. you ll make a star hitch you an yer brother miss. but i guess you win t have no nice smooth bar bite. dey check em an dey bang deir tail an dey bit em de city folk an dey say it s english ye know an dey darsen t cut a horse loose ca se o de cop. n york s no place fer a horse le he s on de belt an can go round wid de boy. wisht i be in de fire department. 
but do you never stop to consider the degradin servitood of it all. say the yellow horse. 
you don t stop on de belt cully. you re stop. an we be all in de servitood business man an horse an jimmy dat sell de paper. guess de passenger weren t out to grass neither by de way dey act. i do my turn an i m none o barnum s crowd. but any horse dat s work on de belt four year don t train wid no simple child o nature not by de whole length o n york. 
but can it be possible that with your experience and at your time of life you do not believe that all horse be free and equal. say the yellow horse. 
not till they re dead muldoon answer quietly. an den it depend on de gross total o button an mucilage dey git out youse at barren island. 
they tell me you re a prominent philosopher. the yellow horse turn to marcus. can you deny a basic and pivotal statement such a this. 
i don t deny anythin say marcus aurelius antoninus cautiously. but ef you ast me i should say twuz more different sort o clip oat of a lie than anythin i ve have my teeth into sence i wuz foal. 
be you a horse. say the yellow horse. 
them that know me best low i be. 
ain t i a horse. 
yep. one kind of. 
then ain t you an me equal. 
how fer kin you go in a day to a load buggy drawin five hundred pound. marcus ask carelessly. 
that ha nothing to do with the case the yellow horse answer excitedly. 
there s nothing i know hez more to do with the case marcus reply. 
kin ye yank a full car out de track ten time in de mornin. say muldoon. 
kin ye go to keene forty two mile in an afternoon with a mate say rick. an turn out bright an early next mornin. 
be there evah any time in your careah suh i be not referrin to the present circumstance but our mutual glorious past when you could carry a pretty girl to market hahnsome an let her knit all the way on account o the smoothness o the motion. say tweezy. 
kin you keep your foot through the west river bridge with the narrer gage comin in on one side an the montreal fly the other an the old bridge teeterin between. say the deacon. kin you put your nose down on the cow catcher of a locomotive when you re waitin at the depot an let em play curfew shall not ring to night with the big brass bell. 
kin you hold back when the brichin break. kin you stop fer order when your nigh hind leg s over your trace an ye feel good of a frosty mornin. say nip who have only learn that trick last winter and think it be the crown of horsely knowledge. 
what s the use o talk in. say tedda gabler scornfully. what kin ye do. 
i rely on my simple right the inalienable right o my unfettered horsehood. an i be proud to say i have never since my first shoe lower myself to obeyin the will o man. 
must ha have a heap o whip break over yer yaller back say tedda. hev ye find it pay any. 
sorrer ha be my portion since the day i be foal. blow an boot an whip an insult injury outrage an oppression. i would not endoor the degradin badge o servitood that connect u with the buggy an the farm wagon. 
it s amazin difficult to draw a buggy thout trace er collar er breast strap er somefin say marcus. a power machine for sawin wood be most the only thing there s no strap to. i ve help saw s much a three cord in an afternoon in a power machine. slep too most o the time i do. but tain t half a interestin ez goin daown taown in the concord. 
concord don t hender you goin to sleep any say nip. my throat lash. d you remember when you lay down in the sharves last week waitin at the piazza. 
pshaw. that didn t hurt the sharves. they wuz good an wide an i lay down keerful. the folk kep me hitch up nigh an hour fore they start. an larfed why they all but lay down themselves with larfin. say boney if you ve get to be hitch to anything that go on wheel you ve get to be hitch with somefin. 
go an jine a circus say muldoon an walk on your hind leg. all de horse dat know too much to work [he pronounce it woik new york fashion] jine de circus. 
i be not sayin anythin again work say the yellow horse. work be the fine thing in the world. 
seem too fine fer some of u tedda snort. 
i only ask that each horse should work for himself an enjoy the profit of his labour. let him work intelligently an not a a machine. 
there ain t no horse that work like a machine marcus begin. 
there s no way o workin that doesn t mean goin to pole er single they never put me in the power machine er under saddle say rick. 
oh shuck. we re talkin same ez we graze say nip raound an raound in circle. rod we hain t hear from you yet an you ve more know how than any span here. 
rod the off horse of the pair have be stand with one hip lift like a tire cow. and you could only tell by the quick flutter of the haw across his eye from time to time that he be pay any attention to the argument. he thrust his jaw out sidewise a his habit be when he pull and change his leg. his voice be hard and heavy and his ear be close to his big plain hambletonian head. 
how old be you. he say to the yellow horse. 
nigh thirteen i guess. 
mean age. ugly age. i m gettin that way myself. how long hev ye be pawin this firefanged stable litter. 
if you mean my principle i ve hold em sence i be three. 
mean age. ugly age. teeth give heap o trouble then. set a colt to actin crazy fer a while. you ve kep it up seemin ly. d ye talk much to your neighbour fer a steady thing. 
i uphold the principle o the cause wherever i be pasture. 
do a heap o good i guess. 
i be proud to say i have teach a few of my companion the principle o freedom an liberty. 
meanin they run away er kick when they get the chanst. 
i be talkin in the abstrac an not in the concrete. my teachin s educate them. 
what a horse specially a young horse hear in the abstrac he s liable to do in the concord. you be handle late i presoom. 
four risin five. 
that s where the trouble begin. driv by a woman like ez not eh. 
not fer long say the yellow horse with a snap of his teeth. 
spill her. 
i heerd she never drive again. 
any childern. 
buckboard full of em. 
men too. 
i have shed conside ble men in my time. 
by kickin. 
any way that come along. fallin back over the dash be a handy a most. 
they must be turr ble afraid o you daown taown. 
they ve send me here to get rid o me. i guess they spend their time talkin over my campaign. 
i wanter know. 
yes sir. now all you gentleman have ask me what i can do. i ll just show you. see them two feller lyin down by the buggy. 
yep. one of em own me. t other break me say rod. 
get em out here in the open an i ll show you something. lemme hide back o you people so s they win t see what i m at. 
meanin ter kill em. rod drawl. there be a shudder of horror through the others. but the yellow horse never notice. 
i ll catch em by the back o the neck an pile drive em a piece. they can suit emselves about livin when i m through with em. 
shouldn t wonder ef they do say rod. the yellow horse have hide himself very cleverly behind the others a they stand in a group and be sway his head close to the grind with a curious scythe like motion look side wise out of his wicked eye. you can never mistake a man eater get ready to knock a man down. we have have one to pasture the year before. 
see that. say my companion turn over on the pine needle. nice for a woman walk cross lot wouldn t it be. 
bring em out. say the yellow horse hunch his sharp back. there s no chance among them tall tree. bring out the oh. ouch. 
it be a right and leave kick from muldoon. i have no idea that the old car horse could lift so quickly. both blow catch the yellow horse full and fair in the rib and knock the breath out of him. 
what s that for. he say angrily when he recover himself. but i notice he do not draw any near to muldoon than be necessary. 
muldoon never answer but discourse to himself in the whine grunt that he us when he be go down hill in front of a heavy load. we call it sing. but i think it s something much bad really. the yellow horse bluster and squeal a little and at last say that if it be a horse fly that have sting muldoon he would accept an apology. 
you ll get it say muldoon in de sweet by and bye all de apology you ve any use for. excuse me interruptin you mr. rod but i m like tweezy i ve a southern drawback in me hind leg. 
naow i want you all here to take notice an you ll learn something rod go on. this yaller back skate come to our pastur. 
not havin pay his board put in tedda. 
not havin earn his board an talk smooth to u abaout ripplin brook an wavin grass an his high tone pure souled horsehood which don t hender him sheddin woman an childern an fallin over the dash onter men. you hear his talk an you think it mighty fine some o you. 
tuck look guilty here but she do not say anything. 
bite by bite he go on ez you have hear. 
i be talkin in the abstrac say the yellow horse in an alter voice. 
abstrac be switch. ez i ve say it s this yer blame abstrac business that make the young un cut up in the concord. an abstrac or no abstrac he crep on an on till he come to killin plain an straight killin them a never do him no harm jest beca se they own horse. 
an know how to manage em say tedda. that make it bad. 
waal he didn t kill em anyway say marcus. he d ha be half kill ef he have try. 
make no differ rod answer. he mean to. an ef he hadn t s pose we want the back pasture turn into a biffin grind on our only day er rest. s pose we want our men walkin round with bit er lead pipe an a twitch an their hand full o stone to throw at u same s if we wuz hog er hooky keows. more n that leavin out tedda here an i guess it s more her maouth than her manner stand in her light there ain t a horse on this farm that ain t a woman s horse an proud of it. an this yer bogspavined kansa sunflower go up an daown the length o the country trade off an trade on boastin a he s shed woman an childern. i don t say a a woman in a buggy ain t a fool. i don t say a she ain t the lastin est kind er fool ner i don t say a child ain t bad spattin the line an standin up an hollerin but i do say tain t none of our business to shed em daown the road. 
we don t say the deacon. the baby try to git some o my tail for a sooveneer last fall when i be up to the haouse an i didn t kick. boney s talk ain t goin to hurt u any. we ain t colt. 
thet s what you think bimeby you git into a tight corner lection day er valley fair like s not daown taown when you re all het an lathery an pester with fly an thirsty an sick o bein work in an aout tween buggy. then somethin whisper inside o your winker bringin up all that talk abaout servitood an inalienable truck an sech like an jest then a militia gun go off. er your wheel hit an waal you re only another horse ez can t be trust. i ve be there time an again. boy fer i ve see you all buy er break on my solemn repitation fer a three minute clip i ain t givin you no bran mash o my own fixin. i m tellin you my experience an i ve have ez heavy a load an ez high a check s any horse here. i wuz bear with a splint on my near fore ez big s a walnut an the cuss three corner hambletonian temper that sour up an curdle daown ez you git old. i ve favour my splint. even little rick he don t know what it s cost me to keep my end up sometimes. an i ve fit my temper in stall an harness hitch up an at pasture till the sweat trickle off my hoof an they think i wuz off condition an drench me. 
when my affliction come say tweezy gently i be very near to losin my manner. allow me to extend to you my sympathy suh. 
rick say nothing but he look at rod curiously. rick be a sunny temper child who never bear malice and i don t think he quite understand. he get his temper from his mother a a horse should. 
i ve be there too rod say tedda. open confession s good for the soul an all monroe county know i ve have my experriences. 
but if you will excuse me suh that pusson tweezy look unspeakable thing at the yellow horse that pusson who ha insult our intelligence come from kansa. an what a ho se of his position an kansa at that say cannot by any stretch of the halter concern gentleman of our position. there s no shadow of equal ty suh not even for one kick. he s beneath our contempt. 
let him talk say marcus. it s always interestin to know what another horse think. it don t tech u. 
an he talk so too say tuck. i ve never hear anythin so smart for a long time. 
again rod stick out his jaw sidewise and go on slowly a though he be slug on a plain bite at the end of a thirty mile drive. 
i want all you here ter understand thet ther ain t no kansa ner no kentucky ner yet no vermont in our business. there s jest two kind o horse in the unite state them ez can an will do their work after bein properly break an handle an them a win t. i m sick an tire o this everlastin tail switchin an wickerin abaout one state er another. a horse kin be proud o his state an swap lie abaout it in stall or when he s hitch to a block ef he keers to put in fly time that way. but he hain t no right to let that pride o hisn interfere with his work ner to make it an excuse fer claimin he s different. that s colt talk an don t you fergit it tweezy. an marcus you remember that hem a philosopher an anxious to save trouble fer you eat don t excuse you from jumpin with all your foot on a slack jaw crazy clay bank like boney here. it s leavin em alone that give em their chance to ruin colt an kill folk. an tuck waal you re a mare anyways but when a horse come along an cover up all his talk o killin with ripplin brook an wavin grass an eight quart of oat a day free after killn his man don t you be run away with by his yap. you re too young an too nervous. 
i ll i ll have nervous prostration sure ef there s a fight here say tuck who saw what be in rod s eye. i m i m that sympathetic i d run away clear to next caounty. 
yep. i know that kind o sympathy. jest last long enough to start a fuss an then light aout to make new trouble. i hain t be ten year in harness fer nuthin. naow we re goin to keep school with boney fer a spell. 
say look a here you ain t goin to hurt me be you. remember i belong to a man in town cry the yellow horse uneasily. muldoon keep behind him so that he could not run away. 
i know it. there must be some pore delooded fool in this state hez a right to the loose end o your hitchin strap. i m blame sorry fer him but he shall hev his right when we re through with you say rod. 
if it s all the same gentleman i d ruther change pasture. guess i ll do it now. 
can t always have your druthers. guess you win t say rod. 
but look a here. all of you ain t so blame unfriendly to a strange. s pose we count nose. 
what in vermont fer. say rod put up his eyebrow. the idea of settle a question by count nose be the very last thing that ever enter the head of a well break horse. 
to see how many s on my side. here s miss tuck anyway. an colonel tweezy yonder s neutral. an judge marcus an i guess the reverend [the yellow horse mean the deacon] might see that i have my right. he s the likeliest lookin trotter i ve ever set eye on. pshaw. boy. you ain t goin to pound me be you. why we ve go round in pasture all colt together this month o sunday hain t we a friendly a could be. there ain t a horse alive i don t care who he be ha a high opinion o you mr. rod than i have. let s do it fair an true an above the exe. let s count nose same s they do in kansa. here he drop his voice a little and turn to marcus. say judge there s some green food i know back o the brook no one hain t touch yet. after this little fracas be fix up you an me ll make up a party an tend to it. 
marcus do not answer for a long time then he say. there s a pup up to the haouse bout eight week old. he ll yap till he git a lickin an when he see it comin he lie on his back an yowl. but he don t go through no cirkituous nose countin first. i ve see a noo light sence rod speak. you ll good stand up to what s serve. i m goin to philosophise all over your carcass. 
i m goin to do yer up in brown paper say muldoon. i can fit you on apology. 
hold on. ef we all biff you now these same men you ve be so dead anxious to kill u d call u off. guess we ll wait till they go back to the haouse an you ll have time to think cool an quiet say rod. 
have you no respec whatever fer the dignity o our common horsehood. the yellow horse squeal. 
nary respec onless the horse kin do something. america s pave with the kind er horse you be jist plain yaller dog horse waitin ter be whip inter shape. we call em yearling an colt when they re young. when they re age we pound em in this pastur. horse sonny be what you start from. we know all about horse here an he ain t any high tone pure souled child o nature. horse plain horse same ez you be chock full o trick an meanness an cussedness an shirkin s an monkey shine which he s take over from his sire an his dam an thicken up with his own special fancy in the way o goin crook. thet s horse an thet s about his dignity an the size of his soul fore he s be break an rawhided a piece. now we ain t goin to give ornery unswitched horse that hain t do nawthin wuth a quart of oat sence he wuz foal pet name that would be good enough fer nancy hank or alix or directum who hev. don t you try to back off acrost them rock. wait where you be. ef i let my hambletonian temper git the good o me i d frazzle you out fine than rye straw inside o three minute you woman scarin kid killin dash breakin unbroke unshod ungaited pastur hoggin saw back shark mouth hair trunk throw in in trade son of a bronco an a sewin machine.

bet your natchul. say muldoon cheerfully and the horse scatter before u trot into the ravine.
